An iterative algorithm repeats a specific calculation, each iteration using the outputs from prior steps as its inputs, and produces a result in each step that converges to the desired value. The approach was actually invented over 160 years earlier by <a href="/wiki/Carl_Friedrich_Gauss" title="Carl Friedrich Gauss">Carl Friedrich Gauss</a>, in what is now termed the <a href="/wiki/AGM_method" title="AGM method">arithmetic–geometric mean method</a> (AGM method) or <a href="/wiki/Gauss%E2%80%93Legendre_algorithm" title="Gauss–Legendre algorithm">Gauss–Legendre algorithm</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Arndt_j_78-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Arndt_j-78"><span>[</span>78<span>]</span></a></sup> As modified by Salamin and Brent, it is also referred to as the Brent–Salamin algorithm.
</p><p>The iterative algorithms were widely used after 1980 because they are faster than infinite series algorithms: whereas infinite series typically increase the number of correct digits additively in successive terms, iterative algorithms generally <i>multiply</i> the number of correct digits at each step. For example, the Brent-Salamin algorithm doubles the number of digits in each iteration. In 1984, the Canadian brothers <a href="/wiki/Jonathan_Borwein" title="Jonathan Borwein">John</a> and <a href="/wiki/Peter_Borwein" title="Peter Borwein">Peter Borwein</a> produced an iterative algorithm that quadruples the number of digits in each step; and in 1987, one that increases the number of digits five times in each step.<sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79"><span>[</span>79<span>]</span></a></sup> Iterative methods were used by Japanese mathematician <a href="/wiki/Yasumasa_Kanada" title="Yasumasa Kanada">Yasumasa Kanada</a> to set several records for computing <span class="texhtml">&#960;</span> between 1995 and 2002.<sup id="cite_ref-Background_80-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Background-80"><span>[</span>80<span>]</span></a></sup> This rapid convergence comes at a price: the iterative algorithms require significantly more memory than infinite series.<sup id="cite_ref-Background_80-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Background-80"><span>[</span>80<span>]</span></a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Motivations_for_computing_.CF.80">Motivations for computing <span class="texhtml">&#960;</span></span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Pi&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Motivations for computing π">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:402px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Record_pi_approximations.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/25/Record_pi_approximations.svg/400px-Record_pi_approximations.svg.png" width="400" height="206" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/25/Record_pi_approximations.svg/600px-Record_pi_approximations.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/25/Record_pi_approximations.svg/800px-Record_pi_approximations.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="1024" data-file-height="528" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Record_pi_approximations.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>As mathematicians discovered new algorithms, and computers became available, the number of known decimal digits of <span class="texhtml">&#960;</span> increased dramatically. Note that the vertical scale is <a href="/wiki/Logarithm" title="Logarithm">logarithmic</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>For most numerical calculations involving <span class="texhtml">&#960;</span>, a handful of digits provide sufficient precision. According to Jörg Arndt and Christoph Haenel, thirty-nine digits are sufficient to perform most <a href="/wiki/Cosmological" title="Cosmological" class="mw-redirect">cosmological</a> calculations, because that is the accuracy necessary to calculate the volume of the known universe with a precision of one atom.<sup id="cite_ref-Arndt_b_81-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Arndt_b-81"><span>[</span>81<span>]</span></a></sup> Despite this, people have worked strenuously to compute <span class="texhtml">&#960;</span> to thousands and millions of digits.<sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82"><span>[</span>82<span>]</span></a></sup> This effort may be partly ascribed to the human compulsion to break records, and such achievements with <span class="texhtml">&#960;</span> often make headlines around the world.<sup id="cite_ref-msnbc.msn.com_83-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-msnbc.msn.com-83"><span>[</span>83<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-independent.co.uk_84-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-independent.co.uk-84"><span>[</span>84<span>]</span></a></sup> They also have practical benefits, such as testing <a href="/wiki/Supercomputer" title="Supercomputer">supercomputers</a>, testing numerical analysis algorithms (including <a href="/wiki/Multiplication_algorithm#Fast_multiplication_algorithms_for_large_inputs" title="Multiplication algorithm">high-precision multiplication algorithms</a>); and within pure mathematics itself, providing data for evaluating the randomness of the digits of <span class="texhtml">&#960;</span>.<sup id="cite_ref-Arndt_c_85-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Arndt_c-85"><span>[</span>85<span>]</span></a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Rapidly_convergent_series">Rapidly convergent series</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Pi&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Rapidly convergent series">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Srinivasa_Ramanujan_-_OPC_-_1.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Photo portrait of a man" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c1/Srinivasa_Ramanujan_-_OPC_-_1.jpg/170px-Srinivasa_Ramanujan_-_OPC_-_1.jpg" width="170" height="233" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c1/Srinivasa_Ramanujan_-_OPC_-_1.jpg/255px-Srinivasa_Ramanujan_-_OPC_-_1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/c/c1/Srinivasa_Ramanujan_-_OPC_-_1.jpg 2x" data-file-width="292" data-file-height="400" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Srinivasa_Ramanujan_-_OPC_-_1.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Srinivasa_Ramanujan" title="Srinivasa Ramanujan">Srinivasa Ramanujan</a>, working in isolation in India, produced many innovative series for computing <span class="texhtml">&#960;</span>.</div></div></div>
<p>Modern <span class="texhtml">&#960;</span> calculators do not use iterative algorithms exclusively. New infinite series were discovered in the 1980s and 1990s that are as fast as iterative algorithms, yet are simpler and less memory intensive.<sup id="cite_ref-Background_80-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Background-80"><span>[</span>80<span>]</span></a></sup> The fast iterative algorithms were anticipated in 1914, when the Indian mathematician <a href="/wiki/Srinivasa_Ramanujan" title="Srinivasa Ramanujan">Srinivasa Ramanujan</a> published dozens of innovative new formulae for <span class="texhtml">&#960;</span>, remarkable for their elegance, mathematical depth, and rapid convergence.<sup id="cite_ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86"><span>[</span>86<span>]</span></a></sup> One of his formulae, based on <a href="/wiki/Modular_equation" title="Modular equation">modular equations</a>, is
</p>
<dl><dd><img class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline tex" alt="\frac{1}{\pi} = \frac{2 \sqrt 2}{9801} \sum_{k=0}^\infty \frac{(4k)!(1103+26390k)}{k!^4(396^{4k})}." src="//upload.wikimedia.org/math/f/6/2/f62b80e3c5cfa1e3281365b89093bce5.png" /></dd></dl>
<p>This series converges much more rapidly than most arctan series, including Machin's formula.<sup id="cite_ref-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-87"><span>[</span>87<span>]</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Bill_Gosper" title="Bill Gosper">Bill Gosper</a> was the first to use it for advances in the calculation of <span class="texhtml">&#960;</span>, setting a record of 17 million digits in 1985.<sup id="cite_ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88"><span>[</span>88<span>]</span></a></sup> Ramanujan's formulae anticipated the modern algorithms developed by the Borwein brothers and the <a href="/wiki/Chudnovsky_brothers" title="Chudnovsky brothers">Chudnovsky brothers</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89"><span>[</span>89<span>]</span></a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Chudnovsky_algorithm" title="Chudnovsky algorithm">Chudnovsky formula</a> developed in 1987 is
</p>
<dl><dd><img class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline tex" alt="\frac{1}{\pi} = \frac{12}{640320^{3/2}} \sum_{k=0}^\infty \frac{(6k)! (13591409 + 545140134k)}{(3k)!(k!)^3 (-640320)^{3k}}." src="//upload.wikimedia.org/math/3/0/c/30c013b91f90b075196b6b964c2c5010.png" /></dd></dl>
<p>It produces about 14 digits of <span class="texhtml">&#960;</span> per term,<sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90"><span>[</span>90<span>]</span></a></sup> and has been used for several record-setting <span class="texhtml">&#960;</span> calculations, including the first to surpass 1 billion (10<sup>9</sup>) digits in 1989 by the Chudnovsky brothers, 2.7 trillion (2.7×10<sup>12</sup>) digits by <a href="/wiki/Fabrice_Bellard" title="Fabrice Bellard">Fabrice Bellard</a> in 2009, and 10 trillion (10<sup>13</sup>) digits in 2011 by Alexander Yee and Shigeru Kondo.<sup id="cite_ref-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-91"><span>[</span>91<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-NW_3-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NW-3"><span>[</span>3<span>]</span></a></sup>  For similar formulas, see also the <a href="/wiki/Ramanujan%E2%80%93Sato_series" title="Ramanujan–Sato series">Ramanujan–Sato series</a>.
</p><p>In 2006, Canadian mathematician <a href="/wiki/Simon_Plouffe" title="Simon Plouffe">Simon Plouffe</a> used the PSLQ <a href="/wiki/Integer_relation_algorithm" title="Integer relation algorithm">integer relation algorithm</a><sup id="cite_ref-92" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-92"><span>[</span>92<span>]</span></a></sup> to generate several new formulas for <span class="texhtml">&#960;</span>, conforming to the following template:
</p>
<dl><dd><img class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline tex" alt="\pi^k = \sum_{n=1}^\infty \frac{1}{n^k} \left(\frac{a}{q^n-1} + \frac{b}{q^{2n}-1} + \frac{c}{q^{4n}-1}\right)," src="//upload.wikimedia.org/math/f/a/2/fa25f93eb50ba556b69590fd6d7bbc85.png" /></dd></dl>
<p>where <img class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline tex" alt="\mathit{q}" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/math/1/a/3/1a301a8476db86fdedf3e72c30036949.png" /> is <a href="/wiki/Gelfond%27s_constant" title="Gelfond&#39;s constant"><span class="texhtml">e</span><sup><span class="texhtml">&#960;</span></sup></a> (Gelfond's constant), <img class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline tex" alt=" \mathit{k}" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/math/6/1/c/61cb5fc1583891b5b592b604950c42bd.png" /> is an <a href="/wiki/Odd_number" title="Odd number" class="mw-redirect">odd number</a>, and <img class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline tex" alt="\mathit{a, b, c}" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/math/1/3/4/134345d1ef75b46a7fd5934d1dfb718e.png" /> are certain rational numbers that Plouffe computed.<sup id="cite_ref-93" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-93"><span>[</span>93<span>]</span></a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Spigot_algorithms">Spigot algorithms</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Pi&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Spigot algorithms">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Two algorithms were discovered in 1995 that opened up new avenues of research into <span class="texhtml">&#960;</span>. They are called <a href="/wiki/Spigot_algorithm" title="Spigot algorithm">spigot algorithms</a> because, like water dripping from a <a href="/wiki/Tap_(valve)" title="Tap (valve)">spigot</a>, they produce single digits of <span class="texhtml">&#960;</span> that are not reused after they are calculated.<sup id="cite_ref-Arndtpp_94-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Arndtpp-94"><span>[</span>94<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Gibbons_95-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gibbons-95"><span>[</span>95<span>]</span></a></sup> This is in contrast to infinite series or iterative algorithms, which retain and use all intermediate digits until the final result is produced.<sup id="cite_ref-Arndtpp_94-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Arndtpp-94"><span>[</span>94<span>]</span></a></sup>
</p><p>American mathematicians <a href="/wiki/Stan_Wagon" title="Stan Wagon">Stan Wagon</a> and Stanley Rabinowitz produced a simple spigot algorithm in 1995.<sup id="cite_ref-Gibbons_95-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gibbons-95"><span>[</span>95<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Arndt_k_96-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Arndt_k-96"><span>[</span>96<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-97" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-97"><span>[</span>97<span>]</span></a></sup> Its speed is comparable to arctan algorithms, but not as fast as iterative algorithms.<sup id="cite_ref-Arndt_k_96-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Arndt_k-96"><span>[</span>96<span>]</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Another spigot algorithm, the <a href="/wiki/Bailey%E2%80%93Borwein%E2%80%93Plouffe_formula" title="Bailey–Borwein–Plouffe formula">BBP</a> <a href="/wiki/Digit_extraction_algorithm" title="Digit extraction algorithm" class="mw-redirect">digit extraction algorithm</a>, was discovered in 1995 by Simon Plouffe:<sup id="cite_ref-Arndtpp_a_98-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Arndtpp_a-98"><span>[</span>98<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-bbpf_99-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bbpf-99"><span>[</span>99<span>]</span></a></sup>
</p>
<dl><dd><img class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline tex" alt=" \pi = \sum_{i=0}^\infty \frac{1}{16^i} \left( \frac{4}{8i + 1} - \frac{2}{8i + 4} - \frac{1}{8i + 5} - \frac{1}{8i + 6}\right)" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/math/8/b/b/8bb0c0c46e24bb426817c4f2a71bdf48.png" /></dd></dl>
<p>This formula, unlike others before it, can produce any individual <a href="/wiki/Hexadecimal" title="Hexadecimal">hexadecimal</a> digit of <span class="texhtml">&#960;</span> without calculating all the preceding digits.<sup id="cite_ref-Arndtpp_a_98-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Arndtpp_a-98"><span>[</span>98<span>]</span></a></sup> Individual binary digits may be extracted from individual hexadecimal digits, and <a href="/wiki/Octal" title="Octal">octal</a> digits can be extracted from one or two hexadecimal digits. Variations of the algorithm have been discovered, but no digit extraction algorithm has yet been found that rapidly produces decimal digits.<sup id="cite_ref-100" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-100"><span>[</span>100<span>]</span></a></sup> An important application of digit extraction algorithms is to validate new claims of record <span class="texhtml">&#960;</span> computations: After a new record is claimed, the decimal result is converted to hexadecimal, and then a digit extraction algorithm is used to calculate several random hexadecimal digits near the end; if they match, this provides a measure of confidence that the entire computation is correct.<sup id="cite_ref-NW_3-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NW-3"><span>[</span>3<span>]</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Between 1998 and 2000, the <a href="/wiki/Distributed_computing" title="Distributed computing">distributed computing</a> project <a href="/wiki/PiHex" title="PiHex">PiHex</a> used <a href="/wiki/Bellard%27s_formula" title="Bellard&#39;s formula">Bellard's formula</a> (a modification of the BBP algorithm) to compute the quadrillionth (10<sup>15</sup>th) bit of <span class="texhtml">&#960;</span>, which turned out to be 0.<sup id="cite_ref-101" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-101"><span>[</span>101<span>]</span></a></sup> In September 2010, a <a href="/wiki/Yahoo!" title="Yahoo!">Yahoo!</a> employee used the company's <a href="/wiki/Apache_Hadoop" title="Apache Hadoop">Hadoop</a> application on one thousand computers over a 23-day period to compute 256 <a href="/wiki/Bit" title="Bit">bits</a> of <span class="texhtml">&#960;</span> at the two-quadrillionth (2×10<sup>15</sup>th) bit, which also happens to be zero.<sup id="cite_ref-102" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-102"><span>[</span>102<span>]</span></a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Use">Use</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Pi&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Use">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">Main article: <a href="/wiki/List_of_formulae_involving_%CF%80" title="List of formulae involving π">List of formulae involving <span class="texhtml">&#960;</span></a></div>
<p>Because <span class="texhtml">&#960;</span> is closely related to the circle, it is found in many formulae from the fields of geometry and trigonometry, particularly those concerning circles, spheres, or ellipses. Formulae from other branches of science also include <span class="texhtml">&#960;</span> in some of their important formulae, including sciences such as statistics, fractals, thermodynamics, mechanics, cosmology, number theory, and electromagnetism.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Geometry_and_trigonometry">Geometry and trigonometry</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Pi&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Geometry and trigonometry">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Circle_Area.svg" class="image"><img alt="A diagram of a circle with a square coving the circle&#39;s upper right quadrant." src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ce/Circle_Area.svg/220px-Circle_Area.svg.png" width="220" height="220" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ce/Circle_Area.svg/330px-Circle_Area.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ce/Circle_Area.svg/440px-Circle_Area.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="265" data-file-height="265" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Circle_Area.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The area of the circle equals <span class="texhtml">&#960;</span> times the shaded area.</div></div></div>
<p><span class="texhtml">&#960;</span> appears in formulae for areas and volumes of geometrical shapes based on circles, such as <a href="/wiki/Ellipse" title="Ellipse">ellipses</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sphere" title="Sphere">spheres</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cone_(geometry)" title="Cone (geometry)" class="mw-redirect">cones</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Torus" title="Torus">tori</a>. Below are some of the more common formulae that involve <span class="texhtml">&#960;</span>.<sup id="cite_ref-103" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-103"><span>[</span>103<span>]</span></a></sup>
</p>
<ul><li> The circumference of a circle with radius <span class="texhtml"><i>r</i></span> is <img class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline tex" alt=" 2 \pi r." src="//upload.wikimedia.org/math/e/3/4/e34be208a81db3596eab60f9c2d1b5ad.png" /></li>
<li> The <a href="/wiki/Area_of_a_disk" title="Area of a disk">area of a circle</a> with radius <span class="texhtml"><i>r</i></span> is <img class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline tex" alt=" \pi r^2." src="//upload.wikimedia.org/math/f/c/8/fc8efaa2b1a80df7d3a7530fef6edb30.png" /></li>
<li> The volume of a sphere with radius <span class="texhtml"><i>r</i></span> is <img class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline tex" alt=" \tfrac43\pi r^3." src="//upload.wikimedia.org/math/e/2/0/e20f86a5187351995c3bd07d17dc1048.png" /></li>
<li> The surface area of a sphere with radius <span class="texhtml"><i>r</i></span> is <img class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline tex" alt=" 4 \pi r^2." src="//upload.wikimedia.org/math/4/e/9/4e9e45a3cddbbf086a93d9646ac158d3.png" /></li></ul>
<p>The formulae above are special cases of the surface area <img class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline tex" alt="S_n(r)" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/math/e/6/4/e648e177c3caa7530b8c4d2ff33add06.png" /> and volume <img class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline tex" alt="V_n(r)" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/math/b/8/2/b82781b8cf276c7e48f157da9df072e2.png" /> of an <a href="/wiki/N-sphere" title="N-sphere"><i>n</i>-dimensional sphere</a>.
</p><p><img class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline tex" alt="S_n(r) = \frac{n\pi^{n/2}}{\Gamma(\frac{n}{2}+1)}r^{n-1}" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/math/b/4/9/b49cc435865d0a791a910f19f684bb29.png" />
</p><p><img class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline tex" alt="V_n(r) = \frac{\pi^{n/2}}{\Gamma(\frac{n}{2}+1)}r^n" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/math/f/6/9/f692c534707a3f1ede90909e52cd5373.png" />
</p><p><span class="texhtml">&#960;</span> appears in <a href="/wiki/Integral" title="Integral">definite integrals</a> that describe circumference, area, or volume of shapes generated by circles. For example, an integral that specifies half the area of a circle of radius one is given by:<sup id="cite_ref-udi_104-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-udi-104"><span>[</span>104<span>]</span></a></sup>
</p>
<dl><dd><img class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline tex" alt="\int_{-1}^1 \sqrt{1-x^2}\,dx = \frac{\pi}{2}." src="//upload.wikimedia.org/math/8/9/4/894cb046549ec6406dd8c532ac338acc.png" /></dd></dl>
<p>In that integral the function <img class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline tex" alt="\scriptstyle \sqrt{1-x^2}" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/math/f/8/f/f8feb7287ff60a9b0bf70ed60e176442.png" /> represents the top half of a circle (the <a href="/wiki/Square_root" title="Square root">square root</a> is a consequence of the <a href="/wiki/Pythagorean_theorem" title="Pythagorean theorem">Pythagorean theorem</a>), and the integral <img class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline tex" alt="\scriptstyle \int_{-1}^1 " src="//upload.wikimedia.org/math/7/e/a/7ea25937247226a6f29f26d716790e30.png" /> computes the area between that half of a circle and the <a href="/wiki/X_axis" title="X axis" class="mw-redirect"><span class="texhtml"><i>x</i></span> axis</a>.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:342px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Sine_cosine_one_period.svg" class="image"><img alt="Diagram showing graphs of functions" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/71/Sine_cosine_one_period.svg/340px-Sine_cosine_one_period.svg.png" width="340" height="136" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/71/Sine_cosine_one_period.svg/510px-Sine_cosine_one_period.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/71/Sine_cosine_one_period.svg/680px-Sine_cosine_one_period.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="600" data-file-height="240" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Sine_cosine_one_period.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Sine" title="Sine">Sine</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cosine" title="Cosine" class="mw-redirect">cosine</a> functions repeat with period 2<span class="texhtml">&#960;</span>.</div></div></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Trigonometric_function" title="Trigonometric function" class="mw-redirect">trigonometric functions</a> rely on angles, and mathematicians generally use radians as units of measurement. <span class="texhtml">&#960;</span> plays an important role in angles measured in <a href="/wiki/Radian" title="Radian">radians</a>, which are defined so that a complete circle spans an angle of 2<span class="texhtml">&#960;</span> radians.<sup id="cite_ref-WR_105-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WR-105"><span>[</span>105<span>]</span></a></sup> The angle measure of 180° is equal to <span class="texhtml">&#960;</span> radians, and 1° = <span class="texhtml">&#960;</span>/180 radians.<sup id="cite_ref-WR_105-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WR-105"><span>[</span>105<span>]</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Common trigonometric functions have periods that are multiples of <span class="texhtml">&#960;</span>; for example, sine and cosine have period 2<span class="texhtml">&#960;</span>,<sup id="cite_ref-WCS_106-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WCS-106"><span>[</span>106<span>]</span></a></sup> so for any angle <i>θ</i> and any integer <span class="texhtml"><i>k</i></span>,
<img class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline tex" alt="\scriptstyle \sin\theta = \sin\left(\theta + 2\pi k \right)" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/math/3/a/1/3a176e7222dadea604130521c6cab930.png" /> and <img class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline tex" alt="\scriptstyle \cos\theta = \cos\left(\theta + 2\pi k \right)." src="//upload.wikimedia.org/math/e/d/0/ed0c5daa24c7ba73eb68916dbcb3c502.png" /><sup id="cite_ref-WCS_106-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WCS-106"><span>[</span>106<span>]</span></a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Monte_Carlo_methods">Monte Carlo methods</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Pi&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Monte Carlo methods">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tmulti tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:274px;max-width:274px"><div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:168px;max-width:168px"><div class="thumbimage"><a href="/wiki/File:Buffon_needle.gif" class="image"><img alt="Needles of length ℓ scattered on stripes with width t" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f6/Buffon_needle.gif/166px-Buffon_needle.gif" width="166" height="100" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/f/f6/Buffon_needle.gif 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/f/f6/Buffon_needle.gif 2x" data-file-width="220" data-file-height="132" /></a></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left"><a href="/wiki/Buffon%27s_needle" title="Buffon&#39;s needle">Buffon's needle</a>. Needles <i>a</i> and <i>b</i> are dropped randomly.</div></div><div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:102px;max-width:102px"><div class="thumbimage"><a href="/wiki/File:Pi_30K.gif" class="image"><img alt="Thousands of dots randomly covering a square and a circle inscribed in the square." src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/84/Pi_30K.gif/100px-Pi_30K.gif" width="100" height="100" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/84/Pi_30K.gif/150px-Pi_30K.gif 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/84/Pi_30K.gif/200px-Pi_30K.gif 2x" data-file-width="500" data-file-height="500" /></a></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">Random dots are placed on the quadrant of a square with a circle inscribed in it.</div></div><div style="clear:left"></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left;text-align:left;background-color:transparent"><a href="/wiki/Monte_Carlo_method" title="Monte Carlo method">Monte Carlo methods</a>, based on random trials, can be used to approximate <span class="texhtml">&#960;</span>.</div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Monte_Carlo_methods" title="Monte Carlo methods" class="mw-redirect">Monte Carlo methods</a>, which evaluate the results of multiple random trials, can be used to create approximations of <span class="texhtml">&#960;</span>.<sup id="cite_ref-107" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-107"><span>[</span>107<span>]</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Buffon%27s_needle" title="Buffon&#39;s needle">Buffon's needle</a> is one such technique: If a needle of length <span class="texhtml"><i>ℓ</i></span> is dropped <span class="texhtml"><i>n</i></span> times on a surface on which parallel lines are drawn <span class="texhtml"><i>t</i></span> units apart, and if <span class="texhtml"><i>x</i></span> of those times it comes to rest crossing a line (<span class="texhtml"><i>x</i></span>&#160;&gt;&#160;0), then one may approximate <span class="texhtml">&#960;</span> based on the counts:<sup id="cite_ref-bn_108-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bn-108"><span>[</span>108<span>]</span></a></sup>
</p>
<dl><dd> <img class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline tex" alt="\pi \approx \frac{2n\ell}{xt}" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/math/d/8/2/d82b22231ed1e97d6f4c927fac0276f8.png" /></dd></dl>
<p>Another Monte Carlo method for computing <span class="texhtml">&#960;</span> is to draw a circle inscribed in a square, and randomly place dots in the square. The ratio of dots inside the circle to the total number of dots will approximately equal <img class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline tex" alt="\scriptstyle \pi/4." src="//upload.wikimedia.org/math/2/6/b/26be66f8bd31e9e099b03ea5d1ad6304.png" /><sup id="cite_ref-109" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-109"><span>[</span>109<span>]</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Monte Carlo methods for approximating <span class="texhtml">&#960;</span> are very slow compared to other methods, and are never used to approximate <span class="texhtml">&#960;</span> when speed or accuracy are desired.<sup id="cite_ref-110" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-110"><span>[</span>110<span>]</span></a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Complex_numbers_and_analysis">Complex numbers and analysis</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Pi&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Complex numbers and analysis">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Euler%27s_formula.svg" class="image"><img alt="A diagram of a unit circle centered at the origin in the complex plane, including a ray from the center of the circle to its edge, with the triangle legs labeled with sine and cosine functions." src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/71/Euler%27s_formula.svg/220px-Euler%27s_formula.svg.png" width="220" height="217" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/71/Euler%27s_formula.svg/330px-Euler%27s_formula.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/71/Euler%27s_formula.svg/440px-Euler%27s_formula.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="760" data-file-height="750" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Euler%27s_formula.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The association between imaginary powers of the number <span class="texhtml"><i>e</i></span> and <a href="/wiki/Point_(geometry)" title="Point (geometry)">points</a> on the <a href="/wiki/Unit_circle" title="Unit circle">unit circle</a> centered at the <a href="/wiki/Origin_(mathematics)" title="Origin (mathematics)">origin</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Complex_plane" title="Complex plane">complex plane</a> given by <a href="/wiki/Euler%27s_formula" title="Euler&#39;s formula">Euler's formula</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>Any <a href="/wiki/Complex_number" title="Complex number">complex number</a>, say <span class="texhtml"><i>z</i></span>, can be expressed using a pair of <a href="/wiki/Real_number" title="Real number">real numbers</a>. In the <a href="/wiki/Polar_coordinate_system#Complex_numbers" title="Polar coordinate system">polar coordinate system</a>, one number (radius or <i>r</i>) is used to represent <span class="texhtml"><i>z</i></span>'s distance from the <a href="/wiki/Origin_(mathematics)" title="Origin (mathematics)">origin</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Complex_plane" title="Complex plane">complex plane</a> and the other (angle or <i>φ</i>) to represent a counter-clockwise <a href="/wiki/Rotation" title="Rotation">rotation</a> from the positive real line as follows:<sup id="cite_ref-111" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-111"><span>[</span>111<span>]</span></a></sup>
</p>
<dl><dd><img class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline tex" alt="z = r\cdot(\cos\varphi + i\sin\varphi)," src="//upload.wikimedia.org/math/c/9/0/c90dbdc50f495b88ad8625ffecd6f3ac.png" /></dd></dl>
<p>where <span class="texhtml"><i>i</i></span> is the <a href="/wiki/Imaginary_unit" title="Imaginary unit">imaginary unit</a> satisfying <span class="texhtml"><i>i</i></span><sup>2</sup> = −1. The frequent appearance of <span class="texhtml">&#960;</span> in <a href="/wiki/Complex_analysis" title="Complex analysis">complex analysis</a> can be related to the behavior of the <a href="/wiki/Exponential_function" title="Exponential function">exponential function</a> of a complex variable, described by <a href="/wiki/Euler%27s_formula" title="Euler&#39;s formula">Euler's formula</a>:<sup id="cite_ref-EF_112-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EF-112"><span>[</span>112<span>]</span></a></sup>
</p>
<dl><dd><img class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline tex" alt="e^{i\varphi} = \cos \varphi + i\sin \varphi," src="//upload.wikimedia.org/math/7/b/f/7bfbc3e2d7df460d7c8f1710b1433db8.png" /></dd></dl>
<p>where <a href="/wiki/E_(mathematical_constant)" title="E (mathematical constant)">the constant <span class="texhtml"><i>e</i></span></a> is the base of the <a href="/wiki/Natural_logarithm" title="Natural logarithm">natural logarithm</a>. This formula establishes a correspondence between imaginary powers of <span class="texhtml"><i>e</i></span> and points on the <a href="/wiki/Unit_circle" title="Unit circle">unit circle</a> centered at the origin of the complex plane. Setting <span class="texhtml"><i>φ</i></span> = <span class="texhtml">&#960;</span> in Euler's formula results in <a href="/wiki/Euler%27s_identity" title="Euler&#39;s identity">Euler's identity</a>, celebrated by mathematicians because it contains the five most important mathematical constants:<sup id="cite_ref-EF_112-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EF-112"><span>[</span>112<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-113" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-113"><span>[</span>113<span>]</span></a></sup>
</p>
<dl><dd><img class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline tex" alt="e^{i \pi} + 1 = 0." src="//upload.wikimedia.org/math/e/4/e/e4eb3aa5e831a682635ceb6bdb1c59d5.png" /></dd></dl>
<p>There are <span class="texhtml"><i>n</i></span> different <a href="/wiki/Complex_number" title="Complex number">complex numbers</a> <span class="texhtml"><i>z</i></span> satisfying <img class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline tex" alt=" z^n = 1" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/math/8/1/f/81f4fb313cd9095b2c6ae8d7c0bb0b4a.png" />, and these are called the "<span class="texhtml"><i>n</i></span>-th <a href="/wiki/Root_of_unity" title="Root of unity">roots of unity</a>".<sup id="cite_ref-114" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-114"><span>[</span>114<span>]</span></a></sup> They are given by this formula:
</p>
<dl><dd><img class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline tex" alt="e^{2 \pi i k/n} \qquad (k = 0, 1, 2, \dots, n - 1)." src="//upload.wikimedia.org/math/a/1/2/a12e91c02ed5aa1d18cf0829c4b13667.png" /></dd></dl>
<p><a href="/wiki/Cauchy%27s_integral_formula" title="Cauchy&#39;s integral formula">Cauchy's integral formula</a> governs <a href="/wiki/Complex_analytic_function" title="Complex analytic function" class="mw-redirect">complex analytic functions</a> and establishes an important relationship between integration and differentiation, including the fact that the values of a complex function within a closed boundary are entirely determined by the values on the boundary:<sup id="cite_ref-115" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-115"><span>[</span>115<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-116" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-116"><span>[</span>116<span>]</span></a></sup>
</p>
<dl><dd><img class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline tex" alt="f (z_{0}) = \frac{1}{ 2\pi i } \oint_\gamma { f(z) \over z-z_0 }\,dz" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/math/b/6/d/b6d25cc07f5880bef67d7968d661210c.png" /></dd></dl>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Mandel_zoom_00_mandelbrot_set.jpg" class="image"><img alt="An complex black shape on a blue background." src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/21/Mandel_zoom_00_mandelbrot_set.jpg/220px-Mandel_zoom_00_mandelbrot_set.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/21/Mandel_zoom_00_mandelbrot_set.jpg/330px-Mandel_zoom_00_mandelbrot_set.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/21/Mandel_zoom_00_mandelbrot_set.jpg/440px-Mandel_zoom_00_mandelbrot_set.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2560" data-file-height="1920" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Mandel_zoom_00_mandelbrot_set.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><span class="texhtml">&#960;</span> can be computed from the <a href="/wiki/Mandelbrot_set" title="Mandelbrot set">Mandelbrot set</a>, by counting the number of iterations required before point (−0.75, ε) diverges.</div></div></div>
<p>An occurrence of <span class="texhtml">&#960;</span> in the <a href="/wiki/Mandelbrot_set" title="Mandelbrot set">Mandelbrot set</a> <a href="/wiki/Fractal" title="Fractal">fractal</a> was discovered by American David Boll in 1991.<sup id="cite_ref-KA_117-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-KA-117"><span>[</span>117<span>]</span></a></sup> He examined the behavior of the Mandelbrot set near the "neck" at (−0.75, 0). If points with coordinates (−0.75, ε) are considered, as ε tends to zero, the number of iterations until divergence for the point multiplied by ε converges to <span class="texhtml">&#960;</span>. The point (0.25, ε) at the cusp of the large "valley" on the right side of the Mandelbrot set behaves similarly: the number of iterations until divergence multiplied by the square root of ε tends to <span class="texhtml">&#960;</span>.<sup id="cite_ref-KA_117-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-KA-117"><span>[</span>117<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-118" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-118"><span>[</span>118<span>]</span></a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Gamma_function" title="Gamma function">gamma function</a> extends the concept of <a href="/wiki/Factorial" title="Factorial">factorial</a>&#160;– which is normally defined only for non-negative integers&#160;– to all complex numbers, except the negative real integers. When the gamma function is evaluated at half-integers, the result contains <span class="texhtml">&#960;</span>; for example <img class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline tex" alt="\scriptstyle \Gamma(1/2) = \sqrt{\pi} " src="//upload.wikimedia.org/math/6/8/e/68eb708918d0b6a0bcdf0246e524a169.png" /> and <img class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline tex" alt="\scriptstyle\Gamma(5/2) = \frac {3 \sqrt{\pi}} {4} " src="//upload.wikimedia.org/math/7/a/4/7a4759671ca58e741a441ab245e4c3eb.png" />.<sup id="cite_ref-119" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-119"><span>[</span>119<span>]</span></a></sup> The gamma function can be used to create a simple approximation to <img class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline tex" alt="\scriptstyle n!" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/math/f/2/6/f26466fe30b03a8b4b49ca0ed28e64fb.png" /> for large <img class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline tex" alt="\scriptstyle n" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/math/9/4/8/9488ed01565ef3fb618b3ccd71349138.png" />: <img class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline tex" alt="\scriptstyle n! \sim \sqrt{2 \pi n} \left(\frac{n}{e}\right)^n" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/math/b/a/6/ba6a94f3943b7310a32fc1be0a6f52d1.png" /> which is known as <a href="/wiki/Stirling%27s_approximation" title="Stirling&#39;s approximation">Stirling's approximation</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-120" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-120"><span>[</span>120<span>]</span></a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Number_theory_and_Riemann_zeta_function">Number theory and Riemann zeta function</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Pi&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Number theory and Riemann zeta function">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Riemann_zeta_function" title="Riemann zeta function">Riemann zeta function</a> <i>ζ</i>(<i>s</i>) is used in many areas of mathematics. When evaluated at <img class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline tex" alt="\scriptstyle s = 2" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/math/f/1/b/f1b88dfc692b86d6d2c23f1d398555a3.png" /> it can be written as
</p>
<dl><dd><img class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline tex" alt=" \zeta(2) = \frac{1}{1^2} + \frac{1}{2^2} + \frac{1}{3^2} + \cdots" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/math/1/a/0/1a02ae25385b67e927b1d67abda871a1.png" /></dd></dl>
<p>Finding a <a href="/wiki/Closed-form_expression" title="Closed-form expression">simple solution</a> for this infinite series was a famous problem in mathematics called the <a href="/wiki/Basel_problem" title="Basel problem">Basel problem</a>. <a href="/wiki/Leonhard_Euler" title="Leonhard Euler">Leonhard Euler</a> solved it in 1735 when he showed it was equal to <img class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline tex" alt="\scriptstyle \pi^2/6" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/math/3/f/4/3f49bae5b6fb1a78d7cca406a91cc292.png" />.<sup id="cite_ref-Posamentier_66-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Posamentier-66"><span>[</span>66<span>]</span></a></sup> Euler's result leads to the <a href="/wiki/Number_theory" title="Number theory">number theory</a> result that the probability of two random numbers being <a href="/wiki/Relatively_prime" title="Relatively prime" class="mw-redirect">relatively prime</a> (that is, having no shared factors) is equal to <img class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline tex" alt="\scriptstyle 6/ \pi^2" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/math/8/6/3/863cf80a6fdf5e10920ea5fda0ba39d2.png" />.<sup id="cite_ref-121" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-121"><span>[</span>121<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-122" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-122"><span>[</span>122<span>]</span></a></sup> This probability is based on the observation that the probability that any number is <a href="/wiki/Divisible" title="Divisible" class="mw-redirect">divisible</a> by a prime <img class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline tex" alt="\scriptstyle p" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/math/9/9/6/99618fd6beeb7e798d84651656d8599c.png" /> is <img class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline tex" alt="\scriptstyle 1/p" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/math/9/4/c/94cb24044dbc77ad66d11d0dc2480565.png" /> (for example, every 7th integer is divisible by 7.) Hence the probability that two numbers are both divisible by this prime is <img class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline tex" alt="\scriptstyle 1/p^2" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/math/d/4/c/d4c89d0425b952a2ed5e6c2fedbc765b.png" />, and the probability that at least one of them is not is <img class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline tex" alt="\scriptstyle 1-1/p^2" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/math/6/9/0/6907dff6ece24423cb56d30f6bc59d4b.png" />. For distinct primes, these divisibility events are mutually independent; so the probability that two numbers are relatively prime is given by a product over all primes:<sup id="cite_ref-123" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-123"><span>[</span>123<span>]</span></a></sup>
</p>
<dl><dd> <img class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline tex" alt="\prod_p^{\infty} \left(1-\frac{1}{p^2}\right) = \left( \prod_p^{\infty} \frac{1}{1-p^{-2}} \right)^{-1} = \frac{1}{1 + \frac{1}{2^2} + \frac{1}{3^2} + \cdots } = \frac{1}{\zeta(2)} = \frac{6}{\pi^2} \approx 61\% " src="//upload.wikimedia.org/math/d/9/1/d91f1ed57835629887fb3e49c552baab.png" /></dd></dl>
<p>This probability can be used in conjunction with a <a href="/wiki/Random_number_generator" title="Random number generator" class="mw-redirect">random number generator</a> to approximate <span class="texhtml">&#960;</span> using a Monte Carlo approach.<sup id="cite_ref-124" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-124"><span>[</span>124<span>]</span></a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Probability_and_statistics">Probability and statistics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Pi&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Probability and statistics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:E%5E(-x%5E2).svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2f/E%5E%28-x%5E2%29.svg/220px-E%5E%28-x%5E2%29.svg.png" width="220" height="176" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2f/E%5E%28-x%5E2%29.svg/330px-E%5E%28-x%5E2%29.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2f/E%5E%28-x%5E2%29.svg/440px-E%5E%28-x%5E2%29.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="600" data-file-height="480" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:E%5E(-x%5E2).svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A graph of the <a href="/wiki/Gaussian_function" title="Gaussian function">Gaussian function</a> <br/> <i>ƒ</i>(<i>x</i>) =&#160;<i>e</i><sup>−<i>x</i><sup>2</sup></sup>. The colored region between the function and the <i>x</i>-axis has area <img class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline tex" alt=" \scriptstyle\sqrt{\pi} " src="//upload.wikimedia.org/math/6/a/0/6a0c08ab1a15984444542182f31f7a51.png" />.</div></div></div>
<p>The fields of <a href="/wiki/Probability" title="Probability">probability</a> and <a href="/wiki/Statistics" title="Statistics">statistics</a> frequently use the <a href="/wiki/Normal_distribution" title="Normal distribution">normal distribution</a> as a simple model for complex phenomena; for example, scientists generally assume that the observational error in most experiments follows a normal distribution.<sup id="cite_ref-125" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-125"><span>[</span>125<span>]</span></a></sup> <span class="texhtml">&#960;</span> is found in the <a href="/wiki/Gaussian_function" title="Gaussian function">Gaussian function</a> (which is the <a href="/wiki/Probability_density_function" title="Probability density function">probability density function</a> of the normal distribution) with <a href="/wiki/Mean" title="Mean">mean</a> <span class="texhtml">μ</span> and <a href="/wiki/Standard_deviation" title="Standard deviation">standard deviation</a> <span class="texhtml">σ</span>:<sup id="cite_ref-GaussProb_126-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-GaussProb-126"><span>[</span>126<span>]</span></a></sup>
</p>
<dl><dd><dl><dd> <img class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline tex" alt="f(x) = {1 \over \sigma\sqrt{2\pi} }\,e^{-(x-\mu )^2/(2\sigma^2)}" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/math/7/c/e/7cebc6d4bfc3a2a33c9af33ca3a2c74a.png" /></dd></dl></dd></dl>
<p>The area under the graph of the normal distribution curve is given by the <a href="/wiki/Gaussian_integral" title="Gaussian integral">Gaussian integral</a>:<sup id="cite_ref-GaussProb_126-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-GaussProb-126"><span>[</span>126<span>]</span></a></sup>
</p>
<dl><dd><img class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline tex" alt="\int_{-\infty}^\infty e^{-x^2} \, dx=\sqrt{\pi}," src="//upload.wikimedia.org/math/9/f/4/9f4637b5394064c2b8c36fb39b44d801.png" /></dd></dl>
<p>while the related integral for the <a href="/wiki/Cauchy_distribution" title="Cauchy distribution">Cauchy distribution</a> is
</p>
<dl><dd><img class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline tex" alt="\int_{-\infty }^{\infty } \frac{1}{x^2+1} \, dx = \pi." src="//upload.wikimedia.org/math/d/e/7/de7f60db6ce4656fa136bfe8401d053e.png" /></dd></dl>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Outside_mathematics">Outside mathematics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Pi&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Outside mathematics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Describing_physical_phenomena">Describing physical phenomena</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Pi&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Describing physical phenomena">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Although not a <a href="/wiki/Physical_constant" title="Physical constant">physical constant</a>, <span class="texhtml">&#960;</span> appears routinely in equations describing fundamental principles of the universe, often because of <span class="texhtml">&#960;</span>'s relationship to the circle and to <a href="/wiki/Spherical_coordinate_system" title="Spherical coordinate system">spherical coordinate systems</a>. A simple formula from the field of <a href="/wiki/Classical_mechanics" title="Classical mechanics">classical mechanics</a> gives the approximate period <span class="texhtml"><i>T</i></span> of a simple <a href="/wiki/Pendulum" title="Pendulum">pendulum</a> of length <span class="texhtml"><i>L</i></span>, swinging with a small amplitude (<span class="texhtml"><i>g</i></span> is the <a href="/wiki/Gravity_of_Earth" title="Gravity of Earth">earth's gravitational acceleration</a>):<sup id="cite_ref-127" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-127"><span>[</span>127<span>]</span></a></sup>
</p>
<dl><dd><img class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline tex" alt="T \approx 2\pi \sqrt\frac{L}{g}." src="//upload.wikimedia.org/math/6/3/0/630be9ddd0a751b76a3976bb50a45656.png" /></dd></dl>
<p>One of the key formulae of <a href="/wiki/Quantum_mechanics" title="Quantum mechanics">quantum mechanics</a> is <a href="/wiki/Uncertainty_principle" title="Uncertainty principle">Heisenberg's uncertainty principle</a>, which shows that the uncertainty in the measurement of a particle's position (Δ<span class="texhtml"><i>x</i></span>) and <a href="/wiki/Momentum" title="Momentum">momentum</a> (Δ<span class="texhtml"><i>p</i></span>) cannot both be arbitrarily small at the same time (where <span class="texhtml"><i>h</i></span> is <a href="/wiki/Planck%27s_constant" title="Planck&#39;s constant" class="mw-redirect">Planck's constant</a>):<sup id="cite_ref-128" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-128"><span>[</span>128<span>]</span></a></sup>
</p>
<dl><dd><img class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline tex" alt=" \Delta x\, \Delta p \ge \frac{h}{4\pi}." src="//upload.wikimedia.org/math/f/8/4/f84b6103d1d3641d50e54683b09c8914.png" /></dd></dl>
<p>In the domain of <a href="/wiki/Cosmology" title="Cosmology">cosmology</a>, <span class="texhtml">&#960;</span> appears in <a href="/wiki/Einstein_field_equations" title="Einstein field equations">Einstein's field equation</a>, a fundamental formula which forms the basis of the <a href="/wiki/General_theory_of_relativity" title="General theory of relativity" class="mw-redirect">general theory of relativity</a> and describes the <a href="/wiki/Fundamental_interaction" title="Fundamental interaction">fundamental interaction</a> of <a href="/wiki/Gravitation" title="Gravitation">gravitation</a> as a result of <a href="/wiki/Spacetime" title="Spacetime">spacetime</a> being <a href="/wiki/Curvature" title="Curvature">curved</a> by <a href="/wiki/Matter" title="Matter">matter</a> and <a href="/wiki/Energy" title="Energy">energy</a>:<sup id="cite_ref-129" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-129"><span>[</span>129<span>]</span></a></sup>
</p>
<dl><dd><img class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline tex" alt=" R_{ik} - {g_{ik} R \over 2} + \Lambda g_{ik} = {8 \pi G \over c^4} T_{ik}," src="//upload.wikimedia.org/math/9/6/5/96584bce7329060cbda46b02ba446bc2.png" /></dd></dl>
<p>where <img class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline tex" alt="R_{ik}\," src="//upload.wikimedia.org/math/7/d/4/7d44c0961e7977027c9327c8a5925f2d.png" /> is the <a href="/wiki/Ricci_curvature_tensor" title="Ricci curvature tensor" class="mw-redirect">Ricci curvature tensor</a>, <img class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline tex" alt="R\," src="//upload.wikimedia.org/math/1/5/3/153fc2a5a0a49d52dda62d96ae0a293f.png" /> is the <a href="/wiki/Scalar_curvature" title="Scalar curvature">scalar curvature</a>, <img class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline tex" alt="g_{ik}\," src="//upload.wikimedia.org/math/1/7/7/177e939a0e8f513705ce14bec73152d1.png" /> is the <a href="/wiki/Metric_tensor_(general_relativity)" title="Metric tensor (general relativity)">metric tensor</a>, <img class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline tex" alt="\Lambda\," src="//upload.wikimedia.org/math/6/0/d/60d25297bc0ea8a83f9cf58edec8e72b.png" /> is the <a href="/wiki/Cosmological_constant" title="Cosmological constant">cosmological constant</a>, <img class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline tex" alt="G\," src="//upload.wikimedia.org/math/3/e/0/3e00f9a1e18c7251df05848cdc0b416b.png" /> is <a href="/wiki/Gravitational_constant" title="Gravitational constant">Newton's gravitational constant</a>, <img class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline tex" alt="c\," src="//upload.wikimedia.org/math/0/8/1/08163b03d3a58471d7f88fc4e581a282.png" /> is the <a href="/wiki/Speed_of_light" title="Speed of light">speed of light</a> in vacuum, and <img class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline tex" alt="T_{ik}\," src="//upload.wikimedia.org/math/6/a/4/6a419967efada9eafd5fb3f40863cb21.png" /> is the <a href="/wiki/Stress%E2%80%93energy_tensor" title="Stress–energy tensor">stress–energy tensor</a>.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Coulomb%27s_law" title="Coulomb&#39;s law">Coulomb's law</a>, from the discipline of <a href="/wiki/Electromagnetism" title="Electromagnetism">electromagnetism</a>, describes the <a href="/wiki/Electric_field" title="Electric field">electric field</a> between two <a href="/wiki/Electric_charge" title="Electric charge">electric charges</a> (<span class="texhtml"><i>q</i></span><sub>1</sub> and <span class="texhtml"><i>q</i></span><sub>2</sub>) separated by distance <span class="texhtml"><i>r</i></span> (with <span class="texhtml">ε</span><sub>0</sub> representing the <a href="/wiki/Vacuum_permittivity" title="Vacuum permittivity">vacuum permittivity</a> of free space):<sup id="cite_ref-130" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-130"><span>[</span>130<span>]</span></a></sup>
</p>
<dl><dd><img class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline tex" alt=" F = \frac{\left|q_1q_2\right|}{4 \pi \varepsilon_0 r^2}." src="//upload.wikimedia.org/math/4/d/4/4d40804e16adfbb2d184355c0f75cf43.png" /></dd></dl>
<p>The fact that <span class="texhtml">&#960;</span> is approximately equal to 3 plays a role in the relatively long lifetime of <a href="/wiki/Positronium" title="Positronium">orthopositronium</a>. The inverse lifetime to lowest order in the fine structure constant <img class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline tex" alt="\alpha" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/math/b/c/c/bccfc7022dfb945174d9bcebad2297bb.png" /> is<sup id="cite_ref-131" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-131"><span>[</span>131<span>]</span></a></sup>
</p>
<dl><dd><img class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline tex" alt="\frac{1}{\tau} = 2\frac{\pi^2 - 9}{9\pi}m\alpha^{6}," src="//upload.wikimedia.org/math/8/f/c/8fc4cc76b8774c980d1d593d6e8d9c73.png" /></dd></dl>
<p>where m is the mass of the electron.
</p><p><span class="texhtml">&#960;</span> is present in some structural engineering formulae, such as the <a href="/wiki/Buckling" title="Buckling">buckling</a> formula derived by Euler, which gives the maximum axial load <span class="texhtml"><i>F</i></span> that a long, slender column of length <span class="texhtml"><i>L</i></span>, <a href="/wiki/Modulus_of_elasticity" title="Modulus of elasticity" class="mw-redirect">modulus of elasticity</a> <span class="texhtml"><i>E</i></span>, and <a href="/wiki/Area_moment_of_inertia" title="Area moment of inertia" class="mw-redirect">area moment of inertia</a> <span class="texhtml"><i>I</i></span> can carry without buckling:<sup id="cite_ref-132" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-132"><span>[</span>132<span>]</span></a></sup>
</p>
<dl><dd><img class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline tex" alt="F =\frac{\pi^2EI}{L^2}." src="//upload.wikimedia.org/math/b/e/f/bef75aeb8af764e5b477b56b1d391f72.png" /></dd></dl>
<p>The field of <a href="/wiki/Fluid_dynamics" title="Fluid dynamics">fluid dynamics</a> contains <span class="texhtml">&#960;</span> in <a href="/wiki/Stokes%27_law" title="Stokes&#39; law">Stokes' law</a>, which approximates the <a href="/wiki/Drag_force" title="Drag force" class="mw-redirect">frictional force</a> F exerted on small, <a href="/wiki/Sphere" title="Sphere">spherical</a> objects of radius <span class="texhtml"><i>R</i></span>, moving with velocity <span class="texhtml"><i>v</i></span> in a <a href="/wiki/Fluid" title="Fluid">fluid</a> with <a href="/wiki/Dynamic_viscosity" title="Dynamic viscosity" class="mw-redirect">dynamic viscosity</a> <i>η</i>:<sup id="cite_ref-133" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-133"><span>[</span>133<span>]</span></a></sup>
</p>
<dl><dd><img class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline tex" alt="F =6 \, \pi \, \eta \, R \, v ." src="//upload.wikimedia.org/math/2/6/9/269d19e45a957ac5d0ff671188f23203.png" /></dd></dl>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Fourier_transform" title="Fourier transform">Fourier transform</a>, defined below, is a mathematical operation that expresses time as a <a href="/wiki/Function_(mathematics)" title="Function (mathematics)">function</a> of <a href="/wiki/Frequency" title="Frequency">frequency</a>, known as its <a href="/wiki/Frequency_spectrum" title="Frequency spectrum">frequency spectrum</a>. It has many applications in <a href="/wiki/Physics" title="Physics">physics</a> and <a href="/wiki/Engineering" title="Engineering">engineering</a>, particularly in <a href="/wiki/Signal_processing" title="Signal processing">signal processing</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-134" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-134"><span>[</span>134<span>]</span></a></sup>
</p>
<dl><dd><img class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline tex" alt="\hat{f}(\xi) = \int_{-\infty}^{\infty} f(x)\ e^{- 2\pi i x \xi}\,dx" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/math/b/7/f/b7f1b12cde784b0cadb5d023179136ed.png" /></dd></dl>
<p>Under ideal conditions (uniform gentle slope on an homogeneously erodible substrate), the <a href="/wiki/Sinuosity" title="Sinuosity">sinuosity</a> of a <a href="/wiki/Meander" title="Meander">meandering</a> river approaches <span class="texhtml">&#960;</span>. The sinuosity is the ratio between the actual length and the straight-line distance from source to mouth. Faster currents along the outside edges of a river's bends cause more erosion than along the inside edges, thus pushing the bends even farther out, and increasing the overall loopiness of the river. However, that loopiness eventually causes the river to double back on itself in places and "short-circuit", creating an <a href="/wiki/Ox-bow_lake" title="Ox-bow lake" class="mw-redirect">ox-bow lake</a> in the process. The balance between these two opposing factors leads to an average ratio of <span class="texhtml">&#960;</span> between the actual length and the direct distance between source and mouth.<sup id="cite_ref-135" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-135"><span>[</span>135<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-136" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-136"><span>[</span>136<span>]</span></a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Memorizing_digits">Memorizing digits</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Pi&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Memorizing digits">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Piphilology" title="Piphilology">Piphilology</a></div>
<p>Many persons have memorized large numbers of digits of <span class="texhtml">&#960;</span>, a practice called <a href="/wiki/Piphilology" title="Piphilology">piphilology</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-A445_137-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-A445-137"><span>[</span>137<span>]</span></a></sup> One common technique is to memorize a story or poem in which the word lengths represent the digits of <span class="texhtml">&#960;</span>: The first word has three letters, the second word has one, the third has four, the fourth has one, the fifth has five, and so on. An early example of a memorization aid, originally devised by English scientist <a href="/wiki/James_Hopwood_Jeans" title="James Hopwood Jeans">James Jeans</a>, is "How I want a drink, alcoholic of course, after the heavy lectures involving quantum mechanics."<sup id="cite_ref-A445_137-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-A445-137"><span>[</span>137<span>]</span></a></sup> When a poem is used, it is sometimes referred to as a <i>piem</i>. Poems for memorizing <span class="texhtml">&#960;</span> have been composed in several languages in addition to English.<sup id="cite_ref-A445_137-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-A445-137"><span>[</span>137<span>]</span></a></sup>
</p><p>The record for memorizing digits of <span class="texhtml">&#960;</span>, certified by <i><a href="/wiki/Guinness_World_Records" title="Guinness World Records">Guinness World Records</a></i>, is 67,890 digits, recited in China by <a href="/wiki/Lu_Chao" title="Lu Chao">Lu Chao</a> in 24 hours and 4 minutes on 20 November 2005.<sup id="cite_ref-138" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-138"><span>[</span>138<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-139" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-139"><span>[</span>139<span>]</span></a></sup> In 2006, <a href="/wiki/Akira_Haraguchi" title="Akira Haraguchi">Akira Haraguchi</a>, a retired Japanese engineer, claimed to have recited 100,000 decimal places, but the claim was not verified by Guinness World Records.<sup id="cite_ref-japantimes_140-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-japantimes-140"><span>[</span>140<span>]</span></a></sup> Record-setting <span class="texhtml">&#960;</span> memorizers typically do not rely on poems, but instead use methods such as remembering number patterns and the <a href="/wiki/Method_of_loci" title="Method of loci">method of loci</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-141" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-141"><span>[</span>141<span>]</span></a></sup>
</p><p>A few authors have used the digits of <span class="texhtml">&#960;</span> to establish a new form of <a href="/wiki/Constrained_writing" title="Constrained writing">constrained writing</a>, where the word lengths are required to represent the digits of <span class="texhtml">&#960;</span>. The <i><a href="/wiki/Cadaeic_Cadenza" title="Cadaeic Cadenza">Cadaeic Cadenza</a></i> contains the first 3835 digits of <span class="texhtml">&#960;</span> in this manner,<sup id="cite_ref-142" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-142"><span>[</span>142<span>]</span></a></sup> and the full-length book <i>Not a Wake</i> contains 10,000 words, each representing one digit of <span class="texhtml">&#960;</span>.<sup id="cite_ref-KeithNAW_143-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-KeithNAW-143"><span>[</span>143<span>]</span></a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="In_popular_culture">In popular culture</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Pi&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: In popular culture">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Pi_pie2.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Pi Pie at Delft University" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d4/Pi_pie2.jpg/220px-Pi_pie2.jpg" width="220" height="220" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d4/Pi_pie2.jpg/330px-Pi_pie2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d4/Pi_pie2.jpg/440px-Pi_pie2.jpg 2x" data-file-width="577" data-file-height="576" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Pi_pie2.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A pi pie. The circular shape of <a href="/wiki/Pie" title="Pie">pie</a> makes it a frequent subject of pi <a href="/wiki/Pun" title="Pun">puns</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>Perhaps because of the simplicity of its definition and its ubiquitous presence in formulae, <span class="texhtml">&#960;</span> has been represented in popular culture more than other mathematical constructs.<sup id="cite_ref-144" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-144"><span>[</span>144<span>]</span></a></sup>
</p><p>In the 2008 <a href="/wiki/Open_University" title="Open University">Open University</a> and <a href="/wiki/BBC" title="BBC">BBC</a> documentary co-production, <a href="/wiki/The_Story_of_Maths" title="The Story of Maths">The Story of Maths</a>, aired in October 2008 on <a href="/wiki/BBC_Four" title="BBC Four">BBC Four</a>, British mathematician <a href="/wiki/Marcus_du_Sautoy" title="Marcus du Sautoy">Marcus du Sautoy</a> shows a <a href="/wiki/Visualization" title="Visualization" class="mw-disambig">visualization</a> of the - historically first exact - <a href="/wiki/Madhava_of_Sangamagrama#The_value_of_.CF.80_.28pi.29" title="Madhava of Sangamagrama">formula for calculating the π</a> when visiting India and exploring its contributions to trigonometry.<sup id="cite_ref-BBC_2008_145-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BBC_2008-145"><span>[</span>145<span>]</span></a></sup>
</p><p>In the <a href="/wiki/Palais_de_la_D%C3%A9couverte" title="Palais de la Découverte">Palais de la Découverte</a> (a science museum in Paris) there is a circular room known as the <i>pi room</i>. On its wall are inscribed 707 digits of <span class="texhtml">&#960;</span>. The digits are large wooden characters attached to the dome-like ceiling. The digits were based on an 1853 calculation by English mathematician <a href="/wiki/William_Shanks" title="William Shanks">William Shanks</a>, which included an error beginning at the 528th digit. The error was detected in 1946 and corrected in 1949.<sup id="cite_ref-146" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-146"><span>[</span>146<span>]</span></a></sup>
</p><p>In <a href="/wiki/Carl_Sagan" title="Carl Sagan">Carl Sagan</a>'s novel <i><a href="/wiki/Contact_(novel)" title="Contact (novel)">Contact</a></i> it is suggested that the creator of the universe buried a message deep within the digits of <span class="texhtml">&#960;</span>.<sup id="cite_ref-147" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-147"><span>[</span>147<span>]</span></a></sup> The digits of <span class="texhtml">&#960;</span> have also been incorporated into the lyrics of the song "Pi" from the album <i><a href="/wiki/Aerial_(album)" title="Aerial (album)">Aerial</a></i> by <a href="/wiki/Kate_Bush" title="Kate Bush">Kate Bush</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-148" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-148"><span>[</span>148<span>]</span></a></sup> and a song by <a href="/wiki/Hard_%27n_Phirm" title="Hard &#39;n Phirm">Hard 'n Phirm</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-149" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-149"><span>[</span>149<span>]</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Many schools in the United States observe <a href="/wiki/Pi_Day" title="Pi Day">Pi Day</a> on 14&#160;March (March is the third month, hence the date is 3/14).<sup id="cite_ref-150" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-150"><span>[</span>150<span>]</span></a></sup> <span class="texhtml">&#960;</span> and its digital representation are often used by self-described "math <a href="/wiki/Geek" title="Geek">geeks</a>" for <a href="/wiki/Inside_joke" title="Inside joke" class="mw-redirect">inside jokes</a> among mathematically and technologically minded groups. Several college <a href="/wiki/Cheering" title="Cheering">cheers</a> at the <a href="/wiki/Massachusetts_Institute_of_Technology" title="Massachusetts Institute of Technology">Massachusetts Institute of Technology</a> include "3.14159".<sup id="cite_ref-MIT_151-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MIT-151"><span>[</span>151<span>]</span></a></sup>
</p><p>During the 2011 auction for <a href="/wiki/Nortel" title="Nortel">Nortel</a>'s portfolio of valuable technology patents, <a href="/wiki/Google" title="Google">Google</a> made a series of unusually specific bids based on mathematical and scientific constants, including <span class="texhtml">&#960;</span>.<sup id="cite_ref-FP_2011_152-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FP_2011-152"><span>[</span>152<span>]</span></a></sup>
</p><p><span id="tau"></span> 
In 1958 <a href="/wiki/Albert_Eagle" title="Albert Eagle">Albert Eagle</a> proposed replacing <span class="texhtml">&#960;</span> by <span class="texhtml">&#964;</span> = <span class="texhtml">&#960;</span>/2 to simplify formulas.<sup id="cite_ref-AE1958_153-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AE1958-153"><span>[</span>153<span>]</span></a></sup> However, no other authors are known to use tau in this way. Some people use a different value for tau, <span class="texhtml">&#964;</span> = 6.283185... = 2<span class="texhtml">&#960;</span>,<sup id="cite_ref-154" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-154"><span>[</span>154<span>]</span></a></sup> arguing that <span class="texhtml">&#964;</span>, as the ratio of a circle's circumference to its radius rather than its diameter, is more natural than <span class="texhtml">&#960;</span> and simplifies many formulas.<sup id="cite_ref-155" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-155"><span>[</span>155<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-156" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-156"><span>[</span>156<span>]</span></a></sup> Celebrations of this number, because it approximately equals 6.28, by making 28&#160;June "Tau Day" and eating "twice the pie",<sup id="cite_ref-157" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-157"><span>[</span>157<span>]</span></a></sup> have been reported in the media. However this use of <span class="texhtml">τ</span> has not made its way into mainstream mathematics.<sup id="cite_ref-158" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-158"><span>[</span>158<span>]</span></a></sup>
</p><p>In 1897, an amateur American mathematician attempted to persuade the <a href="/wiki/Indiana_General_Assembly" title="Indiana General Assembly">Indiana legislature</a> to pass the <a href="/wiki/Indiana_Pi_Bill" title="Indiana Pi Bill">Indiana Pi Bill</a>, which described a method to <a href="/wiki/Squaring_the_circle" title="Squaring the circle">square the circle</a> and contained text that implied various incorrect values for <span class="texhtml">&#960;</span>, including 3.2. The bill is notorious as an attempt to establish a value of scientific constant by legislative fiat. The bill was passed by the Indiana House of Representatives, but rejected by the Senate.<sup id="cite_ref-159" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-159"><span>[</span>159<span>]</span></a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="See_also">See also</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Pi&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: See also">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>

M. F. (1925). "Surveys of the Great Pyramids". <i>Nature Journal</i> <b>116</b> (2930): 942–942. <a href="/wiki/Bibcode" title="Bibcode">Bibcode</a>:<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://adsabs.harvard.edu/abs/1925Natur.116..942P">1925Natur.116..942P</a>. <a href="/wiki/Digital_object_identifier" title="Digital object identifier">doi</a>:<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="//dx.doi.org/10.1038%2F116942a0">10.1038/116942a0</a>.</span><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3APi&amp;rft.atitle=Surveys+of+the+Great+Pyramids&amp;rft.aufirst=W.+M.+F.&amp;rft.aulast=Petrie&amp;rft.au=Petrie%2C+W.+M.+F.&amp;rft.date=1925&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft_id=info%3Abibcode%2F1925Natur.116..942P&amp;rft_id=info%3Adoi%2F10.1038%2F116942a0&amp;rft.issue=2930&amp;rft.jtitle=Nature+Journal&amp;rft.pages=942-942&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.volume=116" class="Z3988"><span style="display:none;">&#160;</span></span></span>

<li id="cite_note-Background-80"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-Background_80-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Background_80-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Background_80-2"><sup><i><b>c</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text"><span class="citation web">Bailey, David H. (16 May 2003). <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://crd-legacy.lbl.gov/~dhbailey/dhbpapers/dhb-kanada.pdf">"Some Background on Kanada’s Recent Pi Calculation"</a><span class="reference-accessdate">. Retrieved <span class="nowrap">12 April</span> 2012</span>.</span><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3APi&amp;rft.au=Bailey%2C+David+H.&amp;rft.aufirst=David+H.&amp;rft.aulast=Bailey&amp;rft.btitle=Some+Background+on+Kanada%E2%80%99s+Recent+Pi+Calculation&amp;rft.date=16+May+2003&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fcrd-legacy.lbl.gov%2F~dhbailey%2Fdhbpapers%2Fdhb-kanada.pdf&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook" class="Z3988"><span style="display:none;">&#160;</span></span></span>

<li id="cite_note-Arndt_b-81"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-Arndt_b_81-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="#CITEREFArndtHaenel2006">Arndt &amp; Haenel 2006</a>, p.&#160;17. "39 digits of <span class="texhtml">&#960;</span> are sufficient to calculate the volume of the universe to the nearest atom."<br/> Accounting for additional digits needed to compensate for computational <a href="/wiki/Round-off_error" title="Round-off error">round-off errors</a>, Arndt concludes that a few hundred digits would suffice for any scientific application.</span>

Bailey">Bailey, David H.</a>; <a href="/wiki/Peter_Borwein" title="Peter Borwein">Borwein, Peter B.</a>; and <a href="/wiki/Simon_Plouffe" title="Simon Plouffe">Plouffe, Simon</a> (April 1997). <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://crd-legacy.lbl.gov/~dhbailey/dhbpapers/digits.pdf">"On the Rapid Computation of Various Polylogarithmic Constants"</a> (PDF). <i>Mathematics of Computation</i> <b>66</b> (218): 903–913. <a href="/wiki/Digital_object_identifier" title="Digital object identifier">doi</a>:<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="//dx.doi.org/10.1090%2FS0025-5718-97-00856-9">10.1090/S0025-5718-97-00856-9</a>.</span><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3APi&amp;rft.atitle=On+the+Rapid+Computation+of+Various+Polylogarithmic+Constants&amp;rft.au=Bailey%2C+David+H.%3B+Borwein%2C+Peter+B.%3B+and+Plouffe%2C+Simon&amp;rft.aulast=Bailey%2C+David+H.%3B+Borwein%2C+Peter+B.%3B+and+Plouffe%2C+Simon&amp;rft.date=April+1997&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fcrd-legacy.lbl.gov%2F~dhbailey%2Fdhbpapers%2Fdigits.pdf&amp;rft_id=info%3Adoi%2F10.1090%2FS0025-5718-97-00856-9&amp;rft.issue=218&amp;rft.jtitle=Mathematics+of+Computation&amp;rft.pages=903-913&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.volume=66" class="Z3988"><span style="display:none;">&#160;</span></span></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-100"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-100">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="#CITEREFArndtHaenel2006">Arndt &amp; Haenel 2006</a>, p.&#160;128. Plouffe did create a decimal digit extraction algorithm, but it is slower than full, direct computation of all preceding digits.</span>
</li>

<li> <a href="/wiki/Srinivasa_Ramanujan" title="Srinivasa Ramanujan">Ramanujan, Srinivasa</a>, "Modular Equations and Approximations to π", <i>Quarterly Journal of Pure and Applied Mathematics</i>, <b>XLV</b>, 1914, 350–372. Reprinted in G.H. Hardy, P.V. Seshu Aiyar, and B. M. Wilson (eds), <i>Srinivasa Ramanujan: Collected Papers</i>, 1927 (reprinted 2000), pp 23–29</li>
<li> <a href="/wiki/William_Shanks" title="William Shanks">Shanks, William</a>, <i>Contributions to Mathematics Comprising Chiefly of the Rectification of the Circle to 607 Places of Decimals</i>, 1853, pp. i–xvi, 10</li>
<li> <a href="/wiki/Daniel_Shanks" title="Daniel Shanks">Shanks, Daniel</a> and <a href="/wiki/John_Wrench" title="John Wrench">Wrench, John William</a>, "Calculation of pi to 100,000 Decimals", <i>Mathematics of Computation</i> <b>16</b>(1962) 76–99</li>
<li> Tropfke, Johannes, <i>Geschichte Der Elementar-Mathematik in Systematischer Darstellung</i> (<i>The history of elementary mathematics</i>), BiblioBazaar, 2009 (reprint), <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/9781113085733" class="internal mw-magiclink-isbn">ISBN 978-1-113-08573-3</a></li>
<li> <a href="/wiki/Fran%C3%A7ois_Vi%C3%A8te" title="François Viète">Viete, Francois</a>, <i>Variorum de Rebus Mathematicis Reponsorum Liber VII. F. Viete, Opera Mathematica</i> (reprint), Georg Olms Verlag, 1970, pp 398–401, 436–446</li>
<li> <a href="/wiki/Stan_Wagon" title="Stan Wagon">Wagon, Stan</a>, "Is Pi Normal?", <i>The Mathematical Intelligencer</i>, <b>7</b>:3(1985) 65–67</li>
<li> <a href="/wiki/John_Wallis" title="John Wallis">Wallis, John</a>, <i>Arithmetica Infinitorum, sive Nova Methodus Inquirendi in Curvilineorum Quadratum, aliaque difficiliora Matheseos Problemata</i>, Oxford 1655–6. Reprinted in vol. 1 (pp 357–478) of <i>Opera Mathematica</i>, Oxford 1693</li>
<li> Zebrowski, Ernest, <i>A History of the Circle: Mathematical Reasoning and the Physical Universe</i>, Rutgers University Press, 1999, <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/9780813528984" class="internal mw-magiclink-isbn">ISBN 978-0-8135-2898-4</a></li></ul>
